<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Chemical reaction</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Process that results in the interconversion of chemical species</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1097763485">.mw-parser-output .ambox{border:1px solid #a2a9b1;border-left:10px solid #36c;background-color:#fbfbfb;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output <p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ThermiteReaction.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2322" data-file-width="2095" decoding="async" height="244" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/ThermiteReaction.jpg/220px-ThermiteReaction.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/ThermiteReaction.jpg/330px-ThermiteReaction.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/ThermiteReaction.jpg/440px-ThermiteReaction.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ThermiteReaction.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Thermite" title="Thermite">thermite</a> reaction using iron(III) oxide. The sparks flying outwards are globules of molten iron trailing smoke in their wake.</div></div></div>
<p>A <b>chemical reaction</b> is a process that leads to the <a href="/wiki/IUPAC_nomenclature_for_organic_transformations" title="IUPAC nomenclature for organic transformations">chemical transformation</a> of one set of <a href="/wiki/Chemical_substance" title="Chemical substance">chemical substances</a> to another.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> Classically, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chemical" title="Chemical">chemical</a> reactions encompass changes that only involve the positions of <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electrons</a> in the forming and breaking of <a href="/wiki/Chemical_bond" title="Chemical bond">chemical bonds</a> between <a href="/wiki/Atom" title="Atom">atoms</a>, with no change to the <a href="/wiki/Atomic_nucleus" title="Atomic nucleus">nuclei</a> (no change to the elements present), and can often be described by a <a href="/wiki/Chemical_equation" title="Chemical equation">chemical equation</a>. <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_chemistry" title="Nuclear chemistry">Nuclear chemistry</a> is a sub-discipline of <a href="/wiki/Chemistry" title="Chemistry">chemistry</a> that involves the chemical reactions of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unstable" title="Unstable">unstable</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radioactive" title="Radioactive">radioactive</a> <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">elements</a> where both electronic and nuclear changes can occur.
</p><p>The substance (or substances) initially involved in a chemical reaction are called <a href="/wiki/Reagent" title="Reagent">reactants or reagents</a>. Chemical reactions are usually characterized by a <a href="/wiki/Chemical_change" title="Chemical change">chemical change</a>, and they yield one or more <a href="/wiki/Product_(chemistry)" title="Product (chemistry)">products</a>, which usually have properties different from the reactants. Reactions often consist of a sequence of individual sub-steps, the so-called <a href="/wiki/Elementary_reaction" title="Elementary reaction">elementary reactions</a>, and the information on the precise course of action is part of the <a href="/wiki/Reaction_mechanism" title="Reaction mechanism">reaction mechanism</a>. Chemical reactions are described with <a href="/wiki/Chemical_equation" title="Chemical equation">chemical equations</a>, which symbolically present the starting materials, end products, and sometimes intermediate products and reaction conditions.
</p><p>Chemical reactions happen at a characteristic <a href="/wiki/Reaction_rate" title="Reaction rate">reaction rate</a> at a given temperature and chemical concentration. Typically, reaction rates increase with increasing temperature because there is more <a href="/wiki/Thermal_energy" title="Thermal energy">thermal energy</a> available to reach the activation energy necessary for breaking bonds between atoms.
Reactions may proceed in the forward or reverse direction until they go to completion or reach <a href="/wiki/Equilibrium_chemistry" title="Equilibrium chemistry">equilibrium</a>. Reactions that proceed in the forward direction to approach equilibrium are often described as <a href="/wiki/Spontaneous_process" title="Spontaneous process">spontaneous</a> and reduce the <a href="/wiki/Gibbs_free_energy" title="Gibbs free energy">free energy</a> if they occur at constant temperature and pressure. Non-spontaneous reactions require input of energy to go forward (examples include charging a battery driven by an external electrical power source, or photosynthesis driven by absorption of <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_radiation" title="Electromagnetic radiation">electromagnetic radiation</a> usually in the form of sunlight).
</p><p>A reaction may be classified as <a href="/wiki/Redox" title="Redox">redox</a> in which <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxidation" title="Oxidation">oxidation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Redox" title="Redox">reduction</a> occur or nonredox in which there is no oxidation and reduction occurring. Most simple redox reactions may be classified as combination, decomposition, or single displacement reactions.
</p><p>Different chemical reactions are used during <a href="/wiki/Chemical_synthesis" title="Chemical synthesis">chemical synthesis</a> in order to obtain a desired product. In <a href="/wiki/Biochemistry" title="Biochemistry">biochemistry</a>, a consecutive series of chemical reactions (where the product of one reaction is the reactant of the next reaction) form <a href="/wiki/Metabolic_pathway" title="Metabolic pathway">metabolic pathways</a>. These reactions are often <a href="/wiki/Catalysis" title="Catalysis">catalyzed</a> by protein <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzymes</a>. Enzymes increase the rates of biochemical reactions, so that <a href="/wiki/Metabolism" title="Metabolism">metabolic</a> syntheses and decompositions impossible under ordinary conditions can occur at the temperature and concentrations present within a <a href="/wiki/Cell_(biology)" title="Cell (biology)">cell</a>.
</p><p>The general concept of a chemical reaction has been extended to reactions between entities smaller than atoms, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_reactions" title="Nuclear reactions">nuclear reactions</a>, <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_decay" title="Radioactive decay">radioactive decays</a>, and reactions between <a href="/wiki/Elementary_particle" title="Elementary particle">elementary particles</a>, as described by <a href="/wiki/Quantum_field_theory" title="Quantum field theory">quantum field theory</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:David_-_Portrait_of_Monsieur_Lavoisier_(cropped).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1266" data-file-width="1097" decoding="async" height="254" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/David_-_Portrait_of_Monsieur_Lavoisier_%28cropped%29.jpg/220px-David_-_Portrait_of_Monsieur_Lavoisier_%28cropped%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/David_-_Portrait_of_Monsieur_Lavoisier_%28cropped%29.jpg/330px-David_-_Portrait_of_Monsieur_Lavoisier_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/David_-_Portrait_of_Monsieur_Lavoisier_%28cropped%29.jpg/440px-David_-_Portrait_of_Monsieur_Lavoisier_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:David_-_Portrait_of_Monsieur_Lavoisier_(cropped).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Antoine_Lavoisier" title="Antoine Lavoisier">Antoine Lavoisier</a> developed the theory of combustion as a chemical reaction with oxygen.</div></div></div>
<p>Chemical reactions such as combustion in fire, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fermentation_(biochemistry)" title="Fermentation (biochemistry)">fermentation</a> and the reduction of ores to metals were known since antiquity. Initial theories of transformation of materials were developed by Greek philosophers, such as the <a href="/wiki/Classical_element" title="Classical element">Four-Element Theory</a> of <a href="/wiki/Empedocles" title="Empedocles">Empedocles</a> stating that any substance is composed of the four basic elements – fire, water, air and earth. In the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>, chemical transformations were studied by <a href="/wiki/Alchemy" title="Alchemy">alchemists</a>. They attempted, in particular, to convert <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a> into <a href="/wiki/Gold" title="Gold">gold</a>, for which purpose they used reactions of lead and lead-copper alloys with <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p><p>The artificial production of chemical substances already was a central goal for medieval alchemists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> Examples include the synthesis of <a href="/wiki/Ammonium_chloride" title="Ammonium chloride">ammonium chloride</a> from <a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">organic substances</a> as described in the works (c. 850–950) attributed to <a href="/wiki/Jabir_ibn_Hayyan" title="Jabir ibn Hayyan">Jābir ibn Ḥayyān</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> or the production of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mineral_acids" title="Mineral acids">mineral acids</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid">sulfuric</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nitric_acid" title="Nitric acid">nitric acids</a> by later alchemists, starting from c. 1300.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> The production of mineral acids involved the heating of sulfate and nitrate minerals such as <a href="/wiki/Copper_sulfate" title="Copper sulfate">copper sulfate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alum" title="Alum">alum</a> and <a href="/wiki/Potassium_nitrate" title="Potassium nitrate">saltpeter</a>. In the 17th century, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Johann_Rudolph_Glauber" title="Johann Rudolph Glauber">Johann Rudolph Glauber</a> produced <a href="/wiki/Hydrochloric_acid" title="Hydrochloric acid">hydrochloric acid</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sodium_sulfate" title="Sodium sulfate">sodium sulfate</a> by reacting sulfuric acid and <a href="/wiki/Sodium_chloride" title="Sodium chloride">sodium chloride</a>. With the development of the <a href="/wiki/Lead_chamber_process" title="Lead chamber process">lead chamber process</a> in 1746 and the <a href="/wiki/Leblanc_process" title="Leblanc process">Leblanc process</a>, allowing large-scale production of sulfuric acid and <a href="/wiki/Sodium_carbonate" title="Sodium carbonate">sodium carbonate</a>, respectively, chemical reactions became implemented into the industry. Further optimization of sulfuric acid technology resulted in the <a href="/wiki/Contact_process" title="Contact process">contact process</a> in the 1880s,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Haber_process" title="Haber process">Haber process</a> was developed in 1909–1910 for <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a> synthesis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>From the 16th century, researchers including <a href="/wiki/Jan_Baptist_van_Helmont" title="Jan Baptist van Helmont">Jan Baptist van Helmont</a>, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Boyle" title="Robert Boyle">Robert Boyle</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Isaac Newton</a> tried to establish theories of the experimentally observed chemical transformations. The <a href="/wiki/Phlogiston_theory" title="Phlogiston theory">phlogiston theory</a> was proposed in 1667 by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/J._J._Becher" title="J. J. Becher">Johann Joachim Becher</a>. It postulated the existence of a fire-like element called "phlogiston", which was contained within combustible bodies and released during <a href="/wiki/Combustion" title="Combustion">combustion</a>. This proved to be false in 1785 by <a href="/wiki/Antoine_Lavoisier" title="Antoine Lavoisier">Antoine Lavoisier</a> who found the correct explanation of the combustion as reaction with oxygen from the air.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Joseph_Louis_Gay-Lussac" title="Joseph Louis Gay-Lussac">Joseph Louis Gay-Lussac</a> recognized in 1808 that gases always react in a certain relationship with each other. Based on this idea and the atomic theory of <a href="/wiki/John_Dalton" title="John Dalton">John Dalton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Proust" title="Joseph Proust">Joseph Proust</a> had developed the <a href="/wiki/Law_of_definite_proportions" title="Law of definite proportions">law of definite proportions</a>, which later resulted in the concepts of <a href="/wiki/Stoichiometry" title="Stoichiometry">stoichiometry</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chemical_equation" title="Chemical equation">chemical equations</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p>Regarding the <a href="/wiki/Organic_chemistry" title="Organic chemistry">organic chemistry</a>, it was long believed that compounds obtained from living organisms were too complex to be obtained <a href="/wiki/Chemical_synthesis" title="Chemical synthesis">synthetically</a>. According to the concept of <a href="/wiki/Vitalism" title="Vitalism">vitalism</a>, organic matter was endowed with a "vital force" and distinguished from inorganic materials. This separation was ended however by the synthesis of <a href="/wiki/Urea" title="Urea">urea</a> from inorganic precursors by <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_W%C3%B6hler" title="Friedrich Wöhler">Friedrich Wöhler</a> in 1828. Other chemists who brought major contributions to organic chemistry include <a href="/wiki/Alexander_William_Williamson" title="Alexander William Williamson">Alexander William Williamson</a> with his <a href="/wiki/Williamson_ether_synthesis" title="Williamson ether synthesis">synthesis</a> of <a href="/wiki/Ether" title="Ether">ethers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Kelk_Ingold" title="Christopher Kelk Ingold">Christopher Kelk Ingold</a>, who, among many discoveries, established the mechanisms of <a href="/wiki/Substitution_reaction" title="Substitution reaction">substitution reactions</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics">Characteristics</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="box-Expand_section plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg"><img alt="[icon]" data-file-height="31" data-file-width="44" decoding="async" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" width="20"/></a></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs expansion</b>. You can help by <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Chemical_reaction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=">adding to it</a>.  <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">November 2020</span>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The general characteristics of chemical reactions are:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Gas_evolution_reaction" title="Gas evolution reaction">Evolution of a gas</a></li>
<li>Formation of a <a href="/wiki/Precipitation_(chemistry)" title="Precipitation (chemistry)">precipitate</a></li>
<li>Change in <a href="/wiki/Temperature" title="Temperature">temperature</a></li>
<li>Change in <a href="/wiki/State_of_matter" title="State of matter">state</a></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Equations">Equations</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:452px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Combustion_reaction_of_methane.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="451" data-file-width="1009" decoding="async" height="201" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Combustion_reaction_of_methane.jpg/450px-Combustion_reaction_of_methane.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Combustion_reaction_of_methane.jpg/675px-Combustion_reaction_of_methane.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Combustion_reaction_of_methane.jpg/900px-Combustion_reaction_of_methane.jpg 2x" width="450"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Combustion_reaction_of_methane.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>As seen from the equation <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1060974363">.mw-parser-output .template-chem2-su{display:inline-block;font-size:80%;line-height:1;vertical-align:-0.35em}.mw-parser-output .template-chem2-su>span{display:block}.mw-parser-output sub.template-chem2-sub{font-size:80%;vertical-align:-0.35em}.mw-parser-output sup.template-chem2-sup{font-size:80%;vertical-align:0.65em}</style><span class="chemf nowrap">CH<sub class="template-chem2-sub">4</sub> + 2O<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub>  →  CO<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub> + 2 H<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub>O</span>, a coefficient of 2 must be placed before the <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> gas on the reactants side and before the <a href="/wiki/Properties_of_water" title="Properties of water">water</a> on the products side in order for, as per the law of conservation of mass, the quantity of each element does not change during the reaction</div></div></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Chemical_equation" title="Chemical equation">Chemical equation</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Chemical_equation" title="Chemical equation">Chemical equations</a> are used to graphically illustrate chemical reactions. They consist of <a href="/wiki/Chemical_formula" title="Chemical formula">chemical</a> or <a href="/wiki/Structural_formula" title="Structural formula">structural formulas</a> of the reactants on the left and those of the products on the right. They are separated by an arrow (→) which indicates the direction and type of the reaction; the arrow is read as the word "yields".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> The tip of the arrow points in the direction in which the reaction proceeds. A double arrow (⇌) pointing in opposite directions is used for <a href="/wiki/Chemical_equilibrium" title="Chemical equilibrium">equilibrium reactions</a>. Equations should be balanced according to the <a href="/wiki/Stoichiometry" title="Stoichiometry">stoichiometry</a>, the number of atoms of each species should be the same on both sides of the equation. This is achieved by scaling the number of involved molecules (A, B, C and D in a schematic example below) by the appropriate integers <i>a, b, c</i> and <i>d</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap"><i>a</i> A + <i>b</i> B  →  <i>c</i> C + <i>d</i> D</span></dd></dl>
<p>More elaborate reactions are represented by reaction schemes, which in addition to starting materials and products show important intermediates or <a href="/wiki/Transition_state" title="Transition state">transition states</a>. Also, some relatively minor additions to the reaction can be indicated above the reaction arrow; examples of such additions are water, heat, illumination, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catalyst" title="Catalyst">catalyst</a>, etc. Similarly, some minor products can be placed below the arrow, often with a minus sign.
</p>
<div class="center"><div class="thumb tnone"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:752px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Baeyer-Villiger-Oxidation-V1.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="186" data-file-width="1568" decoding="async" height="89" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Baeyer-Villiger-Oxidation-V1.svg/750px-Baeyer-Villiger-Oxidation-V1.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Baeyer-Villiger-Oxidation-V1.svg/1125px-Baeyer-Villiger-Oxidation-V1.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Baeyer-Villiger-Oxidation-V1.svg/1500px-Baeyer-Villiger-Oxidation-V1.svg.png 2x" width="750"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Baeyer-Villiger-Oxidation-V1.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An example of organic reaction: <a href="/wiki/Baeyer%E2%80%93Villiger_oxidation" title="Baeyer–Villiger oxidation">oxidation</a> of <a href="/wiki/Ketone" title="Ketone">ketones</a> to <a href="/wiki/Ester" title="Ester">esters</a> with a <a href="/wiki/Peroxy_acid" title="Peroxy acid">peroxycarboxylic acid</a></div></div></div></div>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Retrosynthetic_analysis" title="Retrosynthetic analysis">Retrosynthetic analysis</a> can be applied to design a complex synthesis reaction. Here the analysis starts from the products, for example by splitting selected chemical bonds, to arrive at plausible initial reagents. A special arrow (⇒) is used in retro reactions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Elementary_reactions">Elementary reactions</span></h2>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Elementary_reaction" title="Elementary reaction">elementary reaction</a> is the smallest division into which a chemical reaction can be decomposed, it has no intermediate products.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> Most experimentally observed reactions are built up from many elementary reactions that occur in parallel or sequentially. The actual sequence of the individual elementary reactions is known as <a href="/wiki/Reaction_mechanism" title="Reaction mechanism">reaction mechanism</a>. An elementary reaction involves a few molecules, usually one or two, because of the low probability for several molecules to meet at a certain time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Azobenzene_isomerization_de.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="241" data-file-width="620" decoding="async" height="117" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Azobenzene_isomerization_de.svg/300px-Azobenzene_isomerization_de.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Azobenzene_isomerization_de.svg/450px-Azobenzene_isomerization_de.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Azobenzene_isomerization_de.svg/600px-Azobenzene_isomerization_de.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Azobenzene_isomerization_de.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Isomerization of <a href="/wiki/Azobenzene" title="Azobenzene">azobenzene</a>, induced by light (hν) or heat (Δ)</div></div></div>
<p>The most important elementary reactions are unimolecular and bimolecular reactions. Only one molecule is involved in a unimolecular reaction; it is transformed by an isomerization or a <a href="/wiki/Dissociation_(chemistry)" title="Dissociation (chemistry)">dissociation</a> into one or more other molecules. Such reactions require the addition of energy in the form of heat or light. A typical example of a unimolecular reaction is the <a href="/wiki/Cis%E2%80%93trans_isomerism" title="Cis–trans isomerism">cis–trans</a> <a href="/wiki/Isomerization" title="Isomerization">isomerization</a>, in which the cis-form of a compound converts to the trans-form or vice versa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rh_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-rh-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>In a typical <a href="/wiki/Dissociation_(chemistry)" title="Dissociation (chemistry)">dissociation</a> reaction, a bond in a molecule splits (<b>ruptures</b>) resulting in two molecular fragments. The splitting can be <a href="/wiki/Homolysis_(chemistry)" title="Homolysis (chemistry)">homolytic</a> or <a href="/wiki/Heterolysis_(chemistry)" title="Heterolysis (chemistry)">heterolytic</a>. In the first case, the bond is divided so that each product retains an electron and becomes a neutral <a href="/wiki/Radical_(chemistry)" title="Radical (chemistry)">radical</a>. In the second case, both electrons of the chemical bond remain with one of the products, resulting in charged <a href="/wiki/Ion" title="Ion">ions</a>. Dissociation plays an important role in triggering <a href="/wiki/Chain_reaction" title="Chain reaction">chain reactions</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Oxyhydrogen" title="Oxyhydrogen">hydrogen–oxygen</a> or <a href="/wiki/Polymerization" title="Polymerization">polymerization</a> reactions.
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\ce {AB -&gt; A + B}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mtext>AB</mtext>
<mo stretchy="false">⟶<!-- ⟶ --></mo>
<mtext>A</mtext>
<mo>+</mo>
<mtext>B</mtext>
</mrow>

<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\ce {AB -&gt; A + B}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle {\ce {AB -&gt; A + B}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c95109ff45e4abc6113f26ef442cee98dfad3b73" style="vertical-align: -0.505ex; width:14.714ex; height:2.343ex;"/></span></dd>
<dd><small> Dissociation of a molecule AB into fragments A and B </small></dd></dl>
<p>For <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bimolecular" title="Bimolecular">bimolecular</a> reactions, two molecules collide and react with each other. Their merger is called <a href="/wiki/Chemical_synthesis" title="Chemical synthesis">chemical synthesis</a> or an <a href="/wiki/Addition_reaction" title="Addition reaction">addition reaction</a>.
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\ce {A + B -&gt; AB}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<mtext>A</mtext>
<mo>+</mo>
<mtext>B</mtext>
<mo stretchy="false">⟶<!-- ⟶ --></mo>
<mtext>AB</mtext>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\ce {A + B -&gt; AB}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle {\ce {A + B -&gt; AB}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a430b00af8bf030be7f697aa5392a5e59b947621" style="vertical-align: -0.505ex; width:14.714ex; height:2.343ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>Another possibility is that only a portion of one molecule is transferred to the other molecule. This type of reaction occurs, for example, in <a href="/wiki/Redox" title="Redox">redox</a> and acid–base reactions. In redox reactions, the transferred particle is an electron, whereas in acid–base reactions it is a proton. This type of reaction is also called <a href="/wiki/Salt_metathesis_reaction" title="Salt metathesis reaction">metathesis</a>.
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\ce {HA + B -&gt; A + HB}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</p>

</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle {\ce {NaCl + AgNO3 -&gt; NaNO3 + AgCl(v)}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ebcf421b914c3026008f6a660ac0321ed0836c7c" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; width:37.422ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemical_equilibrium">Chemical equilibrium</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Chemical_equilibrium" title="Chemical equilibrium">Chemical equilibrium</a></div>
<p>Most chemical reactions are reversible; that is, they can and do run in both directions. The forward and reverse reactions are competing with each other and differ in <a href="/wiki/Chemical_kinetics" title="Chemical kinetics">reaction rates</a>. These rates depend on the concentration and therefore change with time of the reaction: the reverse rate gradually increases and becomes equal to the rate of the forward reaction, establishing the so-called chemical equilibrium. The time to reach equilibrium depends on parameters such as temperature, pressure, and the materials involved, and is determined by the <a href="/wiki/Principle_of_minimum_energy" title="Principle of minimum energy">minimum free energy</a>. In equilibrium, the <a href="/wiki/Gibbs_free_energy" title="Gibbs free energy">Gibbs free energy</a> must be zero. The pressure dependence can be explained with the <a href="/wiki/Le_Chatelier%27s_principle" title="Le Chatelier's principle">Le Chatelier's principle</a>. For example, an increase in pressure due to decreasing volume causes the reaction to shift to the side with the fewer moles of gas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>The reaction yield stabilizes at equilibrium, but can be increased by removing the product from the reaction mixture or changed by increasing the temperature or pressure. A change in the concentrations of the reactants does not affect the equilibrium constant, but does affect the equilibrium position.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Thermodynamics">Thermodynamics</span></h2>
<p>Chemical reactions are determined by the laws of <a href="/wiki/Thermodynamics" title="Thermodynamics">thermodynamics</a>. Reactions can proceed by themselves if they are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exergonic" title="Exergonic">exergonic</a>, that is if they release free energy. The associated free energy change of the reaction is composed of the changes of two different thermodynamic quantities, <a href="/wiki/Enthalpy" title="Enthalpy">enthalpy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Entropy" title="Entropy">entropy</a>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><dl><dt><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \Delta G=\Delta H-T\cdot \Delta S}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi mathvariant="normal">Δ<!-- Δ --></mi>
<mi>G</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mi mathvariant="normal">Δ<!-- Δ --></mi>
<mi>H</mi>
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
<mi>T</mi>
<mo>⋅<!-- ⋅ --></mo>
<mi mathvariant="normal">Δ<!-- Δ --></mi>
<mi>S</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \Delta G=\Delta H-T\cdot \Delta S}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle \Delta G=\Delta H-T\cdot \Delta S}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/81f34b838c431f0ec9850a483a6f8a39e34dd8a8" style="vertical-align: -0.505ex; width:20.451ex; height:2.343ex;"/></span>.</dt>
<dd><small> <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">G</span>: free energy, <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">H</span>: enthalpy, <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">T</span>: temperature, <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">S</span>: entropy, <span class="texhtml">Δ</span>: difference (change between original and product) </small></dd></dl></dd></dl>
<p>Reactions can be <a href="/wiki/Exothermic_reaction" title="Exothermic reaction">exothermic</a>, where Δ<i>H</i> is negative and energy is released. Typical examples of exothermic reactions are <a href="/wiki/Combustion" title="Combustion">combustion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Precipitation_(chemistry)" title="Precipitation (chemistry)">precipitation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Crystallization" title="Crystallization">crystallization</a>, in which ordered solids are formed from disordered gaseous or liquid phases. In contrast, in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endothermic" title="Endothermic">endothermic</a> reactions, heat is consumed from the environment. This can occur by increasing the entropy of the system, often through the formation of gaseous or dissolved reaction products, which have higher entropy. Since the entropy term in the free-energy change increases with temperature, many endothermic reactions preferably take place at high temperatures. On the contrary, many exothermic reactions such as crystallization occur preferably at lower temperatures. A change in temperature can sometimes reverse the sign of the enthalpy of a reaction, as for the <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a> reduction of <a href="/wiki/Molybdenum_dioxide" title="Molybdenum dioxide">molybdenum dioxide</a>:
</p>

</p><p>Changes in temperature can also reverse the direction tendency of a reaction. For example, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Water_gas_shift_reaction" title="Water gas shift reaction">water gas shift reaction</a>

The shift in reaction direction tendency occurs at <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7003110000000000000♠"></span>1100 K</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReacWeb_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReacWeb-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>Reactions can also be characterized by their <a href="/wiki/Internal_energy" title="Internal energy">internal energy</a> change, which takes into account changes in the entropy, volume and <a href="/wiki/Chemical_potential" title="Chemical potential">chemical potentials</a>. The latter depend, among other things, on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Activity_(chemistry)" title="Activity (chemistry)">activities</a> of the involved substances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Kinetics">Kinetics</span></h2>
<p>The speed at which reactions takes place is studied by <a href="/wiki/Chemical_kinetics" title="Chemical kinetics">reaction kinetics</a>. The rate depends on various parameters, such as:
</p>
<ul><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reactant" title="Reactant">Reactant</a> concentrations, which usually make the reaction happen at a faster rate if raised through increased collisions per unit time. Some reactions, however, have rates that are <i>independent</i> of reactant concentrations, due to a limited number of catalytic sites.  These are called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rate_law#Zero-order_reactions" title="Rate law">zero order reactions</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Surface_area" title="Surface area">Surface area</a> available for contact between the reactants, in particular solid ones in heterogeneous systems. Larger surface areas lead to higher reaction rates.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Pressure" title="Pressure">Pressure</a> – increasing the pressure decreases the volume between molecules and therefore increases the frequency of collisions between the molecules.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Activation_energy" title="Activation energy">Activation energy</a>, which is defined as the amount of energy required to make the reaction start and carry on spontaneously. Higher activation energy implies that the reactants need more energy to start than a reaction with a lower activation energy.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Temperature" title="Temperature">Temperature</a>, which hastens reactions if raised, since higher temperature increases the energy of the molecules, creating more collisions per unit time,</li>
<li>The presence or absence of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catalyst" title="Catalyst">catalyst</a>. Catalysts are substances which make weak bonds with reactants or intermediates and change the pathway (mechanism) of a reaction which in turn increases the speed of a reaction by lowering the <a href="/wiki/Activation_energy" title="Activation energy">activation energy</a> needed for the reaction to take place. A catalyst is not destroyed or changed during a reaction, so it can be used again.</li>
<li>For some reactions, the presence of <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_radiation" title="Electromagnetic radiation">electromagnetic radiation</a>, most notably <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ultraviolet_light" title="Ultraviolet light">ultraviolet light</a>, is needed to promote the breaking of bonds to start the reaction.  This is particularly true for reactions involving <a href="/wiki/Radical_(chemistry)" title="Radical (chemistry)">radicals</a>.</li></ul>
<p>Several theories allow calculating the reaction rates at the molecular level. This field is referred to as reaction dynamics. The rate <i>v</i> of a <a href="/wiki/Rate_equation#First-order_reactions" title="Rate equation">first-order reaction</a>, which could be disintegration of a substance A, is given by:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle v=-{\frac {d[{\ce {A}}]}{dt}}=k\cdot [{\ce {A}}].}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>v</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mrow>
<mi>d</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">[</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mtext>A</mtext>
</mrow>

</mrow>

</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle v=-{\frac {d[{\ce {A}}]}{dt}}=k\cdot [{\ce {A}}].}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/12291760fcaff20a02ff74abd0dfcb922664cddb" style="vertical-align: -2.005ex; width:20.795ex; height:5.843ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>Its integration yields:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\ce {[A]}}(t)={\ce {[A]}}_{0}\cdot e^{-k\cdot t}.}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

<mo stretchy="false">]</mo>

<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>

<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mo>=</mo>
<msub>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mo stretchy="false">[</mo>
<mtext>A</mtext>
<mo stretchy="false">]</mo>
</mrow>
</mrow>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>0</mn>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>⋅<!-- ⋅ --></mo>
<msup>
<mi>e</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
<mi>k</mi>
<mo>⋅<!-- ⋅ --></mo>
<mi>t</mi>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mo>.</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\ce {[A]}}(t)={\ce {[A]}}_{0}\cdot e^{-k\cdot t}.}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle {\ce {[A]}}(t)={\ce {[A]}}_{0}\cdot e^{-k\cdot t}.}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/498c37558508e2f7297604f93bb5408dcd8c3fd4" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; width:19.703ex; height:3.343ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>Here <i>k</i> is the first-order rate constant, having dimension 1/time, [A](<i>t</i>) is concentration at a time <i>t</i> and [A]<sub>0</sub> is the initial concentration. The rate of a first-order reaction depends only on the concentration and the properties of the involved substance, and the reaction itself can be described with a characteristic <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-life</a>. More than one time constant is needed when describing reactions of higher order. The temperature dependence of the rate constant usually follows the <a href="/wiki/Arrhenius_equation" title="Arrhenius equation">Arrhenius equation</a>:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle k=k_{0}e^{{-E_{a}}/{k_{B}T}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">

<mo>=</mo>
<msub>
<mi>k</mi>

<mn>0</mn>

</msub>
<msup>
<mi>e</mi>

<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
<msub>
<mi>E</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

</mrow>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

</mrow>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<msub>
<mi>k</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

</mrow>
</mrow>

</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle k=k_{0}e^{{-E_{a}}/{k_{B}T}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="k=k_{0}e^{{{-E_{a}}/{k_{{B}}T}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e3a66d123b3069ea7c9928546a43b07a59a143df" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:15.265ex; height:3.176ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>where <i>E</i><sub>a</sub> is the activation energy and <i>k</i><sub>B</sub> is the <a href="/wiki/Boltzmann_constant" title="Boltzmann constant">Boltzmann constant</a>. One of the simplest models of reaction rate is the <a href="/wiki/Collision_theory" title="Collision theory">collision theory</a>. More realistic models are tailored to a specific problem and include the <a href="/wiki/Transition_state_theory" title="Transition state theory">transition state theory</a>, the calculation of the <a href="/wiki/Potential_energy_surface" title="Potential energy surface">potential energy surface</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Marcus_theory" title="Marcus theory">Marcus theory</a> and the <a href="/wiki/RRKM_theory" title="RRKM theory">Rice–Ramsperger–Kassel–Marcus (RRKM) theory</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reaction_types">Reaction types</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Four_basic_types">Four basic types</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:502px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chemical_reactions.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="4000" decoding="async" height="250" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Chemical_reactions.svg/500px-Chemical_reactions.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Chemical_reactions.svg/750px-Chemical_reactions.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Chemical_reactions.svg/1000px-Chemical_reactions.svg.png 2x" width="500"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chemical_reactions.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Representation of four basic chemical reactions types: synthesis, decomposition, single replacement and double replacement.</div></div></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Synthesis">Synthesis</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Synthesis_reaction" title="Synthesis reaction">Synthesis reaction</a></div>
<p>In a synthesis reaction, two or more simple substances combine to form a more complex substance. These reactions are in the general form:
<div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\ce {A + B-&gt;AB}}}" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mtext>A</mtext>
<mo>+</mo>
<mtext>B</mtext>
<mo stretchy="false">⟶<!-- ⟶ --></mo>
<mtext>AB</mtext>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\ce {A + B-&gt;AB}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></div><img alt="{\displaystyle {\ce {A + B-&gt;AB}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-display" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ef2045d38550b4d2b6407af650475a005b345c70" style="vertical-align: -0.505ex; width:14.714ex; height:2.343ex;"/></div>
</p><p>Two or more reactants yielding one product is another way to identify a synthesis reaction. One example of a synthesis reaction is the combination of <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a> to form <a href="/wiki/Iron(II)_sulfide" title="Iron(II) sulfide">iron(II) sulfide</a>:
<div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\ce {8Fe + S8-&gt;8FeS}}}" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>8</mn>
<mspace width="thinmathspace"></mspace>
<mtext>Fe</mtext>
<mo>+</mo>
<msubsup>
<mtext>S</mtext>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>8</mn>
</mrow>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mspace depth=".2em" height="0pt" width="0pt"></mspace>
</mrow>
</msubsup>
<mo stretchy="false">⟶<!-- ⟶ --></mo>
<mn>8</mn>
<mspace width="thinmathspace"></mspace>
<mtext>FeS</mtext>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\ce {8Fe + S8-&gt;8FeS}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></div><img alt="{\displaystyle {\ce {8Fe + S8-&gt;8FeS}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-display" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d4ae465a0cb91e7b9e7bbcafd4167323eca4ebbe" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; width:19.775ex; height:2.843ex;"/></div>
</p><p>Another example is simple hydrogen gas combined with simple oxygen gas to produce a more complex substance, such as water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-to_react_or_not_to_react_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-to_react_or_not_to_react-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Decomposition">Decomposition</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Decomposition_reaction" title="Decomposition reaction">Decomposition reaction</a></div>
<p>A decomposition reaction is when a more complex substance breaks down into its more simple parts. It is thus the opposite of a synthesis reaction, and can be written as<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-to_react_or_not_to_react_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-to_react_or_not_to_react-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chemical_reactions_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-chemical_reactions-22">[22]</a></sup>
<div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\ce {AB-&gt;A + B}}}" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mtext>AB</mtext>
<mo stretchy="false">⟶<!-- ⟶ --></mo>
<mtext>A</mtext>
<mo>+</mo>
<mtext>B</mtext>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\ce {AB-&gt;A + B}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></div><img alt="{\displaystyle {\ce {AB-&gt;A + B}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-display" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/84e927da1a9c126eae7654a01e07ddc94629414f" style="vertical-align: -0.505ex; width:14.714ex; height:2.343ex;"/></div>
</p><p>One example of a decomposition reaction is the <a href="/wiki/Electrolysis" title="Electrolysis">electrolysis</a> of water to make <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> gas:
<div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\ce {2H2O-&gt;2H2 + O2}}}" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

<mo>+</mo>
<msubsup>

</mrow>

</msubsup>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\ce {2H2O-&gt;2H2 + O2}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></div><img alt="{\displaystyle {\ce {2H2O-&gt;2H2 + O2}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-display" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/fbde8ff0c7ba0b5cc6a457f2435b1925caa4df40" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; width:21.301ex; height:2.843ex;"/></div>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Single_displacement">Single displacement</span></h4>
<p>In a <a href="/wiki/Single_displacement_reaction" title="Single displacement reaction">single displacement reaction</a>, a single uncombined element replaces another in a compound; in other words, one element trades places with another element in a compound<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-to_react_or_not_to_react_21-2"><a href="#cite_note-to_react_or_not_to_react-21">[21]</a></sup> These reactions come in the general form of:
<div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\ce {A + BC-&gt;AC + B}}}" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mtext>A</mtext>
<mo>+</mo>
<mtext>BC</mtext>
<mo stretchy="false">⟶<!-- ⟶ --></mo>
<mtext>AC</mtext>
<mo>+</mo>
<mtext>B</mtext>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\ce {A + BC-&gt;AC + B}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></div><img alt="{\displaystyle {\ce {A + BC-&gt;AC + B}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-display" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/3b5f8b379c3df4a1ca7411342cc5454a9b5ae5ef" style="vertical-align: -0.505ex; width:20.91ex; height:2.343ex;"/></div>
</p><p>One example of a single displacement reaction is when <a href="/wiki/Magnesium" title="Magnesium">magnesium</a> replaces hydrogen in water to make <a href="/wiki/Magnesium_hydroxide" title="Magnesium hydroxide">magnesium hydroxide</a> and hydrogen gas:
<div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\ce {Mg + 2H2O-&gt;Mg(OH)2 + H2 (^)}}}" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mtext>Mg</mtext>
<mo>+</mo>
<mn>2</mn>
<mspace width="thinmathspace"></mspace>
<msubsup>

</mrow>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

</mrow>
</msubsup>
<mo>+</mo>
<msubsup>
<mtext>H</mtext>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

</mrow>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

</msubsup>

</mrow>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\ce {Mg + 2H2O-&gt;Mg(OH)2 + H2 (^)}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></div><img alt="{\displaystyle {\ce {Mg + 2H2O-&gt;Mg(OH)2 + H2 (^)}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-display" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/0971ca72969763b007b6927093c1b92806384447" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; width:33.893ex; height:3.009ex;"/></div>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Double_displacement">Double displacement</span></h4>
<p>In a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Double_replacement_reaction" title="Double replacement reaction">double displacement reaction</a>, the anions and cations of two compounds switch places and form two entirely different compounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-to_react_or_not_to_react_21-3"><a href="#cite_note-to_react_or_not_to_react-21">[21]</a></sup> These reactions are in the general form:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chemical_reactions_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-chemical_reactions-22">[22]</a></sup>
<div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\ce {AB + CD-&gt;AD + CB}}}" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mtext>AB</mtext>
<mo>+</mo>
<mtext>CD</mtext>
<mo stretchy="false">⟶<!-- ⟶ --></mo>
<mtext>AD</mtext>
<mo>+</mo>
<mtext>CB</mtext>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\ce {AB + CD-&gt;AD + CB}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></div><img alt="{\displaystyle {\ce {AB + CD-&gt;AD + CB}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-display" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/8dd890b89ea790b908729c64c5a746cdc2c0c6d1" style="vertical-align: -0.505ex; width:24.461ex; height:2.343ex;"/></div>
</p><p>For example, when <a href="/wiki/Barium_chloride" title="Barium chloride">barium chloride</a> (BaCl<sub>2</sub>) and <a href="/wiki/Magnesium_sulfate" title="Magnesium sulfate">magnesium sulfate</a> (MgSO<sub>4</sub>) react, the SO<sub>4</sub><sup>2−</sup> anion switches places with the 2Cl<sup>−</sup> anion, giving the compounds BaSO<sub>4</sub> and MgCl<sub>2</sub>.
</p><p>Another example of a double displacement reaction is the reaction of <a href="/wiki/Lead(II)_nitrate" title="Lead(II) nitrate">lead(II) nitrate</a> with <a href="/wiki/Potassium_iodide" title="Potassium iodide">potassium iodide</a> to form <a href="/wiki/Lead(II)_iodide" title="Lead(II) iodide">lead(II) iodide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Potassium_nitrate" title="Potassium nitrate">potassium nitrate</a>:
<div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\ce {Pb(NO3)2 + 2KI-&gt;PbI2(v) + 2KNO3}}}" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>

</mrow>
</msubsup>

</mrow>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>

</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Combustion">Combustion</span></h3>
<p>In a <a href="/wiki/Combustion" title="Combustion">combustion</a> reaction, an element or compound reacts with an oxidant, usually <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a>, often producing energy in the form of <a href="/wiki/Heat" title="Heat">heat</a> or <a href="/wiki/Light" title="Light">light</a>. Combustion reactions frequently involve a <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">hydrocarbon</a>. For instance, combustion of 1 mole (114 g) of octane in oxygen
<div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\ce {C8H18(l) + 25/2 O2(g)-&gt;8CO2 + 9H2O(l)}}}" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</msubsup>
<mtext>O</mtext>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mtext>l</mtext>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
</mrow>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\ce {C8H18(l) + 25/2 O2(g)-&gt;8CO2 + 9H2O(l)}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></div><img alt="{\displaystyle {\ce {C8H18(l) + 25/2 O2(g)-&gt;8CO2 + 9H2O(l)}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-display" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/5ec0f0d04a1f80c554eb94aa9686bd462616a26e" style="vertical-align: -1.171ex; width:42.6ex; height:3.509ex;"/></div>
</p><p>releases 5500 kJ. A combustion reaction can also result from <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Magnesium" title="Magnesium">magnesium</a> or <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a> reacting with oxygen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup>

</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Oxidation_and_reduction">Oxidation and reduction</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Redox_reaction.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="168" data-file-width="391" decoding="async" height="107" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Redox_reaction.png/250px-Redox_reaction.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Redox_reaction.png/375px-Redox_reaction.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/ee/Redox_reaction.png 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Redox_reaction.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Illustration of a redox reaction</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Common-salt.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1333" data-file-width="2000" decoding="async" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Common-salt.jpg/250px-Common-salt.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Common-salt.jpg/375px-Common-salt.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Common-salt.jpg/500px-Common-salt.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Common-salt.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Sodium_chloride" title="Sodium chloride">Sodium chloride</a> is formed through the redox reaction of sodium metal and chlorine gas</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Redox" title="Redox">Redox</a> reactions can be understood in terms of transfer of electrons from one involved species (<a href="/wiki/Reducing_agent" title="Reducing agent">reducing agent</a>) to another (<a href="/wiki/Oxidizing_agent" title="Oxidizing agent">oxidizing agent</a>). In this process, the former species is <i>oxidized</i> and the latter is <i>reduced</i>. Though sufficient for many purposes, these descriptions are not precisely correct. Oxidation is better defined as an increase in <a href="/wiki/Oxidation_state" title="Oxidation state">oxidation state</a> of atoms, and reduction as a decrease in oxidation state. In practice, the transfer of electrons will always change the oxidation state, but there are many reactions that are classed as "redox" even though no electron transfer occurs (such as those involving <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Covalent" title="Covalent">covalent</a> bonds).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the following redox reaction, hazardous <a href="/wiki/Sodium" title="Sodium">sodium</a> metal reacts with toxic <a href="/wiki/Chlorine" title="Chlorine">chlorine</a> gas to form the ionic compound <a href="/wiki/Sodium_chloride" title="Sodium chloride">sodium chloride</a>, or common table salt:
<div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\ce {2Na(s) + Cl2(g)-&gt;2NaCl(s)}}}" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
<mspace width="thinmathspace"></mspace>
<mtext>Na</mtext>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mtext>s</mtext>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
</mrow>
<mo>+</mo>
<msubsup>
<mtext>Cl</mtext>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mspace depth=".2em" height="0pt" width="0pt"></mspace>
</mrow>
</msubsup>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mtext>g</mtext>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
</mrow>
<mo stretchy="false">⟶<!-- ⟶ --></mo>
<mn>2</mn>
<mspace width="thinmathspace"></mspace>
<mtext>NaCl</mtext>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mtext>s</mtext>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
</mrow>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\ce {2Na(s) + Cl2(g)-&gt;2NaCl(s)}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></div><img alt="{\displaystyle {\ce {2Na(s) + Cl2(g)-&gt;2NaCl(s)}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-display" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/fd530a39ca1cdf827c6461404b0ff495bde68652" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; width:30.973ex; height:3.009ex;"/></div>
</p><p>In the reaction, sodium metal goes from an oxidation state of 0 (as it is a pure element) to +1: in other words, the sodium lost one electron and is said to have been oxidized. On the other hand, the chlorine gas goes from an oxidation of 0 (it is also a pure element) to −1: the chlorine gains one electron and is said to have been reduced. Because the chlorine is the one reduced, it is considered the electron acceptor, or in other words, induces oxidation in the sodium – thus the chlorine gas is considered the oxidizing agent. Conversely, the sodium is oxidized or is the electron donor, and thus induces reduction in the other species and is considered the <i>reducing agent</i>.
</p><p>Which of the involved reactants would be reducing or oxidizing agent can be predicted from the <a href="/wiki/Electronegativity" title="Electronegativity">electronegativity</a> of their elements. Elements with low electronegativity, such as most metals, easily donate electrons and oxidize – they are reducing agents. On the contrary, many oxides or ions with high oxidation numbers of their non-oxygen atoms, such as <span class="chemf nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_peroxide" title="Hydrogen peroxide">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></a></span>, <span class="chemf nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Permanganate" title="Permanganate">MnO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></a></span>, <span class="chemf nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Chromium_trioxide" title="Chromium trioxide">CrO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></a></span>, <span class="chemf nowrap"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dichromate" title="Dichromate">Cr<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">7</sub></span></a></span>, or <span class="chemf nowrap"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Osmium(VIII)_oxide" title="Osmium(VIII) oxide">OsO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></a></span>, can gain one or two extra electrons and are strong oxidizing agents.
</p><p>For some <a href="/wiki/Main-group_element" title="Main-group element">main-group elements</a> the number of electrons donated or accepted in a redox reaction can be predicted from the <a href="/wiki/Electron_configuration" title="Electron configuration">electron configuration</a> of the reactant element. Elements try to reach the low-energy <a href="/wiki/Noble_gas" title="Noble gas">noble gas</a> configuration, and therefore alkali metals and halogens will donate and accept one electron, respectively. Noble gases themselves are chemically inactive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>The overall redox reaction <a href="/wiki/Redox#Balancing_redox_reactions" title="Redox">can be balanced</a> by combining the oxidation and reduction half-reactions multiplied by coefficients such that the number of electrons lost in the oxidation equals the number of electrons gained in the reduction.
</p><p>An important class of redox reactions are the electrolytic <a href="/wiki/Electrochemistry" title="Electrochemistry">electrochemical</a> reactions, where electrons from the power supply at the negative electrode are used as the reducing agent, and electron withdrawal at the positive electrode as the oxidizing agent. These reactions are particularly important for the production of chemical elements, such as <a href="/wiki/Chlorine" title="Chlorine">chlorine</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> or <a href="/wiki/Aluminium" title="Aluminium">aluminium</a>. The reverse process, in which electrons are released in redox reactions and chemical energy is converted to electrical energy, is possible and used in <a href="/wiki/Electric_battery" title="Electric battery">batteries</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Complexation">Complexation</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:162px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ferrocene-from-xtal-3D-balls.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1100" data-file-width="1002" decoding="async" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Ferrocene-from-xtal-3D-balls.png/160px-Ferrocene-from-xtal-3D-balls.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Ferrocene-from-xtal-3D-balls.png/240px-Ferrocene-from-xtal-3D-balls.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Ferrocene-from-xtal-3D-balls.png/320px-Ferrocene-from-xtal-3D-balls.png 2x" width="160"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ferrocene-from-xtal-3D-balls.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ferrocene" title="Ferrocene">Ferrocene</a> – an iron atom sandwiched between two C<sub>5</sub>H<sub>5</sub> <a href="/wiki/Ligand" title="Ligand">ligands</a></div></div></div>
<p>In complexation reactions, several <a href="/wiki/Ligand" title="Ligand">ligands</a> react with a metal atom to form a <a href="/wiki/Coordination_complex" title="Coordination complex">coordination complex</a>. This is achieved by providing <a href="/wiki/Lone_pair" title="Lone pair">lone pairs</a> of the ligand into empty <a href="/wiki/Atomic_orbital" title="Atomic orbital">orbitals</a> of the metal atom and forming <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dipolar_bond" title="Dipolar bond">dipolar bonds</a>. The ligands are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lewis_base" title="Lewis base">Lewis bases</a>, they can be both ions and neutral molecules, such as carbon monoxide, ammonia or water. The number of ligands that react with a central metal atom can be found using the <a href="/wiki/18-electron_rule" title="18-electron rule">18-electron rule</a>, saying that the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Valence_shell" title="Valence shell">valence shells</a> of a <a href="/wiki/Transition_metal" title="Transition metal">transition metal</a> will collectively accommodate 18 <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electrons</a>, whereas the symmetry of the resulting complex can be predicted with the <a href="/wiki/Crystal_field_theory" title="Crystal field theory">crystal field theory</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ligand_field_theory" title="Ligand field theory">ligand field theory</a>. Complexation reactions also include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ligand_exchange" title="Ligand exchange">ligand exchange</a>, in which one or more ligands are replaced by another, and redox processes which change the oxidation state of the central metal atom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Acid.E2.80.93base_reactions"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Acid–base_reactions">Acid–base reactions</span></h3>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Br%C3%B8nsted%E2%80%93Lowry_acid%E2%80%93base_theory" title="Brønsted–Lowry acid–base theory">Brønsted–Lowry acid–base theory</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Acid%E2%80%93base_reaction" title="Acid–base reaction">acid–base reaction</a> involves a transfer of <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">protons</a> (H<sup>+</sup>) from one species (the <a href="/wiki/Acid" title="Acid">acid</a>) to another (the <a href="/wiki/Base_(chemistry)" title="Base (chemistry)">base</a>). When a proton is removed from an acid, the resulting species is termed that acid's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conjugate_acid" title="Conjugate acid">conjugate base</a>. When the proton is accepted by a base, the resulting species is termed that base's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conjugate_acid" title="Conjugate acid">conjugate acid</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> In other words, acids act as proton donors and bases act as proton acceptors according to the following equation:
<div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\ce {{\underset {acid}{HA}}+{\underset {base}{B}}&lt;=&gt;{\underset {conjugated\ base}{A^{-}}}+{\underset {conjugated\ acid}{HB+}}}}}" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

<mtext>acid</mtext>
</munder>
</mrow>
<mo>+</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<munder>
<mtext>B</mtext>
<mtext>base</mtext>
</munder>
</mrow>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-REL">
<mover>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-OP MJX-fixedlimits">

<mpadded depth="0" height="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

</mrow>

<mspace width="negativethinmathspace"></mspace>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
</mrow>
</mpadded>
</mrow>
</mrow>
