</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\ce {{\underset {acid}{HA}}+{\underset {base}{B}}&lt;=&gt;{\underset {conjugated\ base}{A^{-}}}+{\underset {conjugated\ acid}{HB+}}}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></div><img alt="{\displaystyle {\ce {{\underset {acid}{HA}}+{\underset {base}{B}}&lt;=&gt;{\underset {conjugated\ base}{A^{-}}}+{\underset {conjugated\ acid}{HB+}}}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-display" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c1dbe5e51cc9337e3395e1df902be7386a03191e" style="vertical-align: -2.338ex; width:39.749ex; height:4.676ex;"/></div>
</p><p>The reverse reaction is possible, and thus the acid/base and conjugated base/acid are always in equilibrium. The equilibrium is determined by the <a href="/wiki/Acid_dissociation_constant" title="Acid dissociation constant">acid and base dissociation constants</a> (<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> and <i>K</i><sub>b</sub>) of the involved substances. A special case of the acid–base reaction is the <a href="/wiki/Neutralization_(chemistry)" title="Neutralization (chemistry)">neutralization</a> where an acid and a base, taken at exactly same amounts, form a neutral <a href="/wiki/Salt_(chemistry)" title="Salt (chemistry)">salt</a>.
</p><p>Acid–base reactions can have different definitions depending on the acid–base concept employed. Some of the most common are:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Acid%E2%80%93base_reaction#Arrhenius_definition" title="Acid–base reaction">Arrhenius</a> definition: Acids dissociate in water releasing H<sub>3</sub>O<sup>+</sup> ions; bases dissociate in water releasing OH<sup>−</sup> ions.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Br%C3%B8nsted%E2%80%93Lowry_acid%E2%80%93base_theory" title="Brønsted–Lowry acid–base theory">Brønsted–Lowry</a> definition: Acids are proton (H<sup>+</sup>) donors, bases are proton acceptors; this includes the Arrhenius definition.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Acid%E2%80%93base_reaction#Lewis_definition" title="Acid–base reaction">Lewis</a> definition: Acids are electron-pair acceptors, bases are electron-pair donors; this includes the Brønsted-Lowry definition.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Precipitation">Precipitation</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chemical_precipitation_diagram_multilang.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="550" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="202" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Chemical_precipitation_diagram_multilang.svg/220px-Chemical_precipitation_diagram_multilang.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Chemical_precipitation_diagram_multilang.svg/330px-Chemical_precipitation_diagram_multilang.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Chemical_precipitation_diagram_multilang.svg/440px-Chemical_precipitation_diagram_multilang.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chemical_precipitation_diagram_multilang.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Precipitation</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Precipitation_(chemistry)" title="Precipitation (chemistry)">Precipitation</a> is the formation of a solid in a solution or inside another solid during a chemical reaction. It usually takes place when the concentration of dissolved ions exceeds the <a href="/wiki/Solubility" title="Solubility">solubility</a> limit<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> and forms an insoluble salt. This process can be assisted by adding a precipitating agent or by removal of the solvent. Rapid precipitation results in an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amorphous" title="Amorphous">amorphous</a> or microcrystalline residue and slow process can yield single <a href="/wiki/Crystal" title="Crystal">crystals</a>. The latter can also be obtained by <a href="/wiki/Recrystallization_(chemistry)" title="Recrystallization (chemistry)">recrystallization</a> from microcrystalline salts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Solid-state_reactions">Solid-state reactions</span></h3>
<p>Reactions can take place between two solids. However, because of the relatively small <a href="/wiki/Diffusion" title="Diffusion">diffusion</a> rates in solids, the corresponding chemical reactions are very slow in comparison to liquid and gas phase reactions. They are accelerated by increasing the reaction temperature and finely dividing the reactant to increase the contacting surface area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Reactions_at_the_solid.2Fgas_interface"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Reactions_at_the_solid/gas_interface">Reactions at the solid/gas interface</span></h3>
<p>Reaction can take place at the solid|gas interface, surfaces at very low pressure such as <a href="/wiki/Ultra-high_vacuum" title="Ultra-high vacuum">ultra-high vacuum</a>. Via <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scanning_tunneling_microscopy" title="Scanning tunneling microscopy">scanning tunneling microscopy</a>, it is possible to observe reactions at the solid|gas interface in real space, if the time scale of the reaction is in the correct range.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> Reactions at the solid|gas interface are in some cases related to catalysis.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Photochemical_reactions">Photochemical reactions</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Paterno-Buchi_reaction.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="200" data-file-width="700" decoding="async" height="63" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Paterno-Buchi_reaction.svg/220px-Paterno-Buchi_reaction.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Paterno-Buchi_reaction.svg/330px-Paterno-Buchi_reaction.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Paterno-Buchi_reaction.svg/440px-Paterno-Buchi_reaction.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Paterno-Buchi_reaction.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>In this <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paterno%E2%80%93B%C3%BCchi_reaction" title="Paterno–Büchi reaction">Paterno–Büchi reaction</a>, a photoexcited carbonyl group is added to an unexcited <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Olefin" title="Olefin">olefin</a>, yielding an <a href="/wiki/Oxetane" title="Oxetane">oxetane</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Photochemistry" title="Photochemistry">photochemical reactions</a>, atoms and molecules absorb energy (<a href="/wiki/Photon" title="Photon">photons</a>) of the illumination light and convert into an <a href="/wiki/Excited_state" title="Excited state">excited state</a>. They can then release this energy by breaking chemical bonds, thereby producing radicals. Photochemical reactions include hydrogen–oxygen reactions, <a href="/wiki/Radical_polymerization" title="Radical polymerization">radical polymerization</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chain_reaction" title="Chain reaction">chain reactions</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rearrangement_reaction" title="Rearrangement reaction">rearrangement reactions</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many important processes involve photochemistry. The premier example is <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesis</a>, in which most plants use solar energy to convert <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> and water into <a href="/wiki/Glucose" title="Glucose">glucose</a>, disposing of <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> as a side-product. Humans rely on photochemistry for the formation of vitamin D, and <a href="/wiki/Visual_perception" title="Visual perception">vision</a> is initiated by a photochemical reaction of <a href="/wiki/Rhodopsin" title="Rhodopsin">rhodopsin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rh_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-rh-15">[15]</a></sup> In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fireflies" title="Fireflies">fireflies</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzyme</a> in the abdomen catalyzes a reaction that results in <a href="/wiki/Bioluminescence" title="Bioluminescence">bioluminescence</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> Many significant photochemical reactions, such as ozone formation, occur in the Earth atmosphere and constitute <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_chemistry" title="Atmospheric chemistry">atmospheric chemistry</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Catalysis">Catalysis</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Catalysis" title="Catalysis">Catalysis</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reaction_Progress_Kinetic_Analysis" title="Reaction Progress Kinetic Analysis">Reaction Progress Kinetic Analysis</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Activation_energy.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="743" data-file-width="929" decoding="async" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Activation_energy.svg/220px-Activation_energy.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Activation_energy.svg/330px-Activation_energy.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Activation_energy.svg/440px-Activation_energy.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Activation_energy.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Schematic potential energy diagram showing the effect of a catalyst in an endothermic chemical reaction. The presence of a catalyst opens a different reaction pathway (in red) with a lower activation energy. The final result and the overall thermodynamics are the same.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pot_catalytique_vue_de_la_structure.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1217" data-file-width="2068" decoding="async" height="129" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Pot_catalytique_vue_de_la_structure.jpg/220px-Pot_catalytique_vue_de_la_structure.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Pot_catalytique_vue_de_la_structure.jpg/330px-Pot_catalytique_vue_de_la_structure.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Pot_catalytique_vue_de_la_structure.jpg/440px-Pot_catalytique_vue_de_la_structure.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pot_catalytique_vue_de_la_structure.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Solid heterogeneous catalysts are plated on meshes in ceramic <a href="/wiki/Catalytic_converter" title="Catalytic converter">catalytic converters</a> in order to maximize their surface area. This exhaust converter is from a <a href="/wiki/Peugeot_106" title="Peugeot 106">Peugeot 106</a> S2 1100</div></div></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Catalysis" title="Catalysis">catalysis</a>, the reaction does not proceed directly, but through reaction with a third substance known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catalyst" title="Catalyst">catalyst</a>. Although the catalyst takes part in the reaction, forming weak bonds with reactants or intermediates, it is returned to its original state by the end of the reaction and so is not consumed. However, it can be inhibited, deactivated or destroyed by secondary processes. Catalysts can be used in a different phase (<a href="/wiki/Heterogeneous_catalysis" title="Heterogeneous catalysis">heterogeneous</a>) or in the same phase (<a href="/wiki/Homogeneous_catalysis" title="Homogeneous catalysis">homogeneous</a>) as the reactants. In heterogeneous catalysis, typical secondary processes include <a href="/wiki/Coking" title="Coking">coking</a> where the catalyst becomes covered by <a href="/wiki/Polymer" title="Polymer">polymeric</a> side products. Additionally, heterogeneous catalysts can dissolve into the solution in a solid–liquid system or evaporate in a solid–gas system. Catalysts can only speed up the reaction – chemicals that slow down the reaction are called inhibitors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> Substances that increase the activity of catalysts are called promoters, and substances that deactivate catalysts are called catalytic poisons. With a catalyst, a reaction which is kinetically inhibited by a high activation energy can take place in circumvention of this activation energy.
</p><p>Heterogeneous catalysts are usually solids, powdered in order to maximize their surface area. Of particular importance in heterogeneous catalysis are the <a href="/wiki/Platinum_group" title="Platinum group">platinum group</a> metals and other transition metals, which are used in <a href="/wiki/Hydrogenation" title="Hydrogenation">hydrogenations</a>, <a href="/wiki/Catalytic_reforming" title="Catalytic reforming">catalytic reforming</a> and in the synthesis of commodity chemicals such as <a href="/wiki/Nitric_acid" title="Nitric acid">nitric acid</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a>. Acids are an example of a homogeneous catalyst, they increase the nucleophilicity of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbonyl" title="Carbonyl">carbonyls</a>, allowing a reaction that would not otherwise proceed with electrophiles. The advantage of homogeneous catalysts is the ease of mixing them with the reactants, but they may also be difficult to separate from the products. Therefore, heterogeneous catalysts are preferred in many industrial processes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reactions_in_organic_chemistry">Reactions in organic chemistry</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Organic_reaction" title="Organic reaction">Organic reaction</a></div>
<p>In organic chemistry, in addition to oxidation, reduction or acid–base reactions, a number of other reactions can take place which involve <a href="/wiki/Covalent_bond" title="Covalent bond">covalent bonds</a> between carbon atoms or carbon and <a href="/wiki/Heteroatom" title="Heteroatom">heteroatoms</a> (such as oxygen, nitrogen, <a href="/wiki/Halogen" title="Halogen">halogens</a>, etc.). Many specific reactions in organic chemistry are <a href="/wiki/Name_reaction" title="Name reaction">name reactions</a> designated after their discoverers.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Substitution">Substitution</span></h3>
<p>In a <a href="/wiki/Substitution_reaction" title="Substitution reaction">substitution reaction</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Functional_group" title="Functional group">functional group</a> in a particular <a href="/wiki/Chemical_compound" title="Chemical compound">chemical compound</a> is replaced by another group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jerry_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-jerry-40">[40]</a></sup> These reactions can be distinguished by the type of substituting species into a <a href="/wiki/Nucleophilic_substitution" title="Nucleophilic substitution">nucleophilic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Electrophilic_substitution" title="Electrophilic substitution">electrophilic</a> or <a href="/wiki/Radical_substitution" title="Radical substitution">radical substitution</a>.
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .multiimageinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{display:flex;flex-direction:row;clear:left;flex-wrap:wrap;width:100%;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{margin:1px;float:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .theader{clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;align-self:center;background-color:transparent;width:100%}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-left{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-right{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-center{text-align:center}@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;max-width:none!important;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{justify-content:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle .thumbcaption{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow>.thumbcaption{text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:304px;max-width:304px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:302px;max-width:302px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SN1_reaction_mechanism.png"><img alt="" data-file-height="476" data-file-width="1074" decoding="async" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/SN1_reaction_mechanism.png/300px-SN1_reaction_mechanism.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/SN1_reaction_mechanism.png/450px-SN1_reaction_mechanism.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/SN1_reaction_mechanism.png/600px-SN1_reaction_mechanism.png 2x" width="300"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">S<sub>N</sub>1 mechanism</div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:302px;max-width:302px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SN2_reaction_mechanism.png"><img alt="" data-file-height="261" data-file-width="1487" decoding="async" height="53" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/SN2_reaction_mechanism.png/300px-SN2_reaction_mechanism.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/SN2_reaction_mechanism.png/450px-SN2_reaction_mechanism.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/SN2_reaction_mechanism.png/600px-SN2_reaction_mechanism.png 2x" width="300"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">S<sub>N</sub>2 mechanism</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>In the first type, a <a href="/wiki/Nucleophile" title="Nucleophile">nucleophile</a>, an atom or molecule with an excess of electrons and thus a negative charge or <a href="/wiki/Partial_charge" title="Partial charge">partial charge</a>, replaces another atom or part of the "substrate" molecule. The electron pair from the nucleophile attacks the substrate forming a new bond, while the <a href="/wiki/Leaving_group" title="Leaving group">leaving group</a> departs with an electron pair. The nucleophile may be electrically neutral or negatively charged, whereas the substrate is typically neutral or positively charged. Examples of nucleophiles are <a href="/wiki/Hydroxide" title="Hydroxide">hydroxide</a> ion, <a href="/wiki/Alkoxide" title="Alkoxide">alkoxides</a>, <a href="/wiki/Amine" title="Amine">amines</a> and <a href="/wiki/Halide" title="Halide">halides</a>. This type of reaction is found mainly in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aliphatic_hydrocarbon" title="Aliphatic hydrocarbon">aliphatic hydrocarbons</a>, and rarely in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aromatic_hydrocarbon" title="Aromatic hydrocarbon">aromatic hydrocarbon</a>. The latter have high electron density and enter <a href="/wiki/Nucleophilic_aromatic_substitution" title="Nucleophilic aromatic substitution">nucleophilic aromatic substitution</a> only with very strong <a href="/wiki/Polar_effect" title="Polar effect">electron withdrawing groups</a>. Nucleophilic substitution can take place by two different mechanisms, <a href="/wiki/SN1_reaction" title="SN1 reaction">S<sub>N</sub>1</a> and <a href="/wiki/SN2_reaction" title="SN2 reaction">S<sub>N</sub>2</a>. In their names, S stands for substitution, N for nucleophilic, and the number represents the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Order_(chemistry)" title="Order (chemistry)">kinetic order</a> of the reaction, unimolecular or bimolecular.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:124px;max-width:124px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:122px;max-width:122px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Walden-inversion-3D-balls.png"><img alt="" data-file-height="2150" data-file-width="1318" decoding="async" height="196" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Walden-inversion-3D-balls.png/120px-Walden-inversion-3D-balls.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Walden-inversion-3D-balls.png/180px-Walden-inversion-3D-balls.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Walden-inversion-3D-balls.png/240px-Walden-inversion-3D-balls.png 2x" width="120"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">The three steps of an <a href="/wiki/SN2_reaction" title="SN2 reaction">S<sub>N</sub>2 reaction</a>. The nucleophile is green and the leaving group is red</div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:122px;max-width:122px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SN2-Walden-before-and-after-horizontal-3D-balls.png"><img alt="" data-file-height="529" data-file-width="1100" decoding="async" height="58" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/SN2-Walden-before-and-after-horizontal-3D-balls.png/120px-SN2-Walden-before-and-after-horizontal-3D-balls.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/SN2-Walden-before-and-after-horizontal-3D-balls.png/180px-SN2-Walden-before-and-after-horizontal-3D-balls.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/SN2-Walden-before-and-after-horizontal-3D-balls.png/240px-SN2-Walden-before-and-after-horizontal-3D-balls.png 2x" width="120"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">S<sub>N</sub>2 reaction causes stereo inversion (Walden inversion)</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>The S<sub>N</sub>1 reaction proceeds in two steps. First, the <a href="/wiki/Leaving_group" title="Leaving group">leaving group</a> is eliminated creating a <a href="/wiki/Carbocation" title="Carbocation">carbocation</a>. This is followed by a rapid reaction with the nucleophile.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the S<sub>N</sub>2 mechanism, the nucleophile forms a transition state with the attacked molecule, and only then the leaving group is cleaved. These two mechanisms differ in the <a href="/wiki/Stereochemistry" title="Stereochemistry">stereochemistry</a> of the products. S<sub>N</sub>1 leads to the non-stereospecific addition and does not result in a chiral center, but rather in a set of <a href="/wiki/Cis%E2%80%93trans_isomerism" title="Cis–trans isomerism">geometric isomers</a> (<i>cis/trans</i>). In contrast, a reversal (<a href="/wiki/Walden_inversion" title="Walden inversion">Walden inversion</a>) of the previously existing stereochemistry is observed in the S<sub>N</sub>2 mechanism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Electrophilic_substitution" title="Electrophilic substitution">Electrophilic substitution</a> is the counterpart of the nucleophilic substitution in that the attacking atom or molecule, an <a href="/wiki/Electrophile" title="Electrophile">electrophile</a>, has low electron density and thus a positive charge. Typical electrophiles are the carbon atom of <a href="/wiki/Carbonyl_group" title="Carbonyl group">carbonyl groups</a>, carbocations or <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nitronium" title="Nitronium">nitronium</a> cations. This reaction takes place almost exclusively in aromatic hydrocarbons, where it is called <a href="/wiki/Electrophilic_aromatic_substitution" title="Electrophilic aromatic substitution">electrophilic aromatic substitution</a>. The electrophile attack results in the so-called σ-complex, a transition state in which the aromatic system is abolished. Then, the leaving group, usually a proton, is split off and the aromaticity is restored. An alternative to aromatic substitution is electrophilic aliphatic substitution. It is similar to the nucleophilic aliphatic substitution and also has two major types, S<sub>E</sub>1 and S<sub>E</sub>2<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="center"><div class="thumb tnone"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:650px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Electrophilic_aromatic_substitution.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="297" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="301" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Electrophilic_aromatic_substitution.svg/648px-Electrophilic_aromatic_substitution.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Electrophilic_aromatic_substitution.svg/972px-Electrophilic_aromatic_substitution.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Electrophilic_aromatic_substitution.svg/1296px-Electrophilic_aromatic_substitution.svg.png 2x" width="648"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Electrophilic_aromatic_substitution.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mechanism of electrophilic aromatic substitution</div></div></div></div>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<p>In the third type of substitution reaction, radical substitution, the attacking particle is a <a href="/wiki/Radical_(chemistry)" title="Radical (chemistry)">radical</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jerry_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-jerry-40">[40]</a></sup> This process usually takes the form of a <a href="/wiki/Chain_reaction" title="Chain reaction">chain reaction</a>, for example in the reaction of alkanes with halogens. In the first step, light or heat disintegrates the halogen-containing molecules producing the radicals. Then the reaction proceeds as an avalanche until two radicals meet and recombine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p>

<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\ce {X. + R-H -&gt; X-H + R.}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle {\ce {X. + R-H -&gt; X-H + R.}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/07b98ce5cde918e9f9a84463020095c3b5af1869" style="vertical-align: -0.505ex; width:26.08ex; height:2.343ex;"/></span></dt>

<dd><small> Reactions during the chain reaction of radical substitution </small></dd></dl></dd></dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Addition_and_elimination">Addition and elimination</span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Addition_reaction" title="Addition reaction">addition</a> and its counterpart, the <a href="/wiki/Elimination_reaction" title="Elimination reaction">elimination</a>, are reactions which change the number of substituents on the carbon atom, and form or cleave <a href="/wiki/Covalent_bond" title="Covalent bond">multiple bonds</a>. <a href="/wiki/Double_bond" title="Double bond">Double</a> and <a href="/wiki/Triple_bond" title="Triple bond">triple bonds</a> can be produced by eliminating a suitable leaving group. Similar to the nucleophilic substitution, there are several possible reaction mechanisms which are named after the respective reaction order. In the E1 mechanism, the leaving group is ejected first, forming a carbocation. The next step, formation of the double bond, takes place with elimination of a proton (<a href="/wiki/Deprotonation" title="Deprotonation">deprotonation</a>). The leaving order is reversed in the E1cb mechanism, that is the proton is split off first. This mechanism requires participation of a base.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> Because of the similar conditions, both reactions in the E1 or E1cb elimination always compete with the S<sub>N</sub>1 substitution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tnone center"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:808px;max-width:808px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:402px;max-width:402px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:E1-mechanism.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="119" data-file-width="626" decoding="async" height="76" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/E1-mechanism.svg/400px-E1-mechanism.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/E1-mechanism.svg/600px-E1-mechanism.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/E1-mechanism.svg/800px-E1-mechanism.svg.png 2x" width="400"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">E1 elimination</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:402px;max-width:402px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:E1cb-mechanism.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="206" data-file-width="683" decoding="async" height="121" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/E1cb-mechanism.svg/400px-E1cb-mechanism.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/E1cb-mechanism.svg/600px-E1cb-mechanism.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/E1cb-mechanism.svg/800px-E1cb-mechanism.svg.png 2x" width="400"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">E1cb elimination</div></div></div></div></div>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:E2-mechanism.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="212" data-file-width="451" decoding="async" height="141" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/E2-mechanism.svg/300px-E2-mechanism.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/E2-mechanism.svg/450px-E2-mechanism.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/E2-mechanism.svg/600px-E2-mechanism.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:E2-mechanism.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>E2 elimination</div></div></div>
<p>The E2 mechanism also requires a base, but there the attack of the base and the elimination of the leaving group proceed simultaneously and produce no ionic intermediate. In contrast to the E1 eliminations, different stereochemical configurations are possible for the reaction product in the E2 mechanism, because the attack of the base preferentially occurs in the anti-position with respect to the leaving group. Because of the similar conditions and reagents, the E2 elimination is always in competition with the S<sub>N</sub>2-substitution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:HBr-addition.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="221" data-file-width="1384" decoding="async" height="48" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/HBr-addition.svg/300px-HBr-addition.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/HBr-addition.svg/450px-HBr-addition.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/HBr-addition.svg/600px-HBr-addition.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:HBr-addition.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Electrophilic addition of hydrogen bromide</div></div></div>
<p>The counterpart of elimination is the addition where double or triple bonds are converted into single bonds. Similar to the substitution reactions, there are several types of additions distinguished by the type of the attacking particle. For example, in the <a href="/wiki/Electrophilic_addition" title="Electrophilic addition">electrophilic addition</a> of hydrogen bromide, an electrophile (proton) attacks the double bond forming a <a href="/wiki/Carbocation" title="Carbocation">carbocation</a>, which then reacts with the nucleophile (bromine). The carbocation can be formed on either side of the double bond depending on the groups attached to its ends, and the preferred configuration can be predicted with the <a href="/wiki/Markovnikov%27s_rule" title="Markovnikov's rule">Markovnikov's rule</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> This rule states that "In the heterolytic addition of a polar molecule to an alkene or alkyne, the more electronegative (nucleophilic) atom (or part) of the polar molecule becomes attached to the carbon atom bearing the smaller number of hydrogen atoms."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p>If the addition of a functional group takes place at the less substituted carbon atom of the double bond, then the electrophilic substitution with acids is not possible. In this case, one has to use the <a href="/wiki/Hydroboration%E2%80%93oxidation_reaction" title="Hydroboration–oxidation reaction">hydroboration–oxidation reaction</a>, where in the first step, the <a href="/wiki/Boron" title="Boron">boron</a> atom acts as electrophile and adds to the less substituted carbon atom. At the second step, the nucleophilic <a href="/wiki/Hydroperoxide" title="Hydroperoxide">hydroperoxide</a> or halogen <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anion" title="Anion">anion</a> attacks the boron atom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p>While the addition to the electron-rich alkenes and alkynes is mainly electrophilic, the <a href="/wiki/Nucleophilic_addition" title="Nucleophilic addition">nucleophilic addition</a> plays an important role for the carbon-heteroatom multiple bonds, and especially its most important representative, the carbonyl group. This process is often associated with an elimination, so that after the reaction the carbonyl group is present again. It is therefore called addition-elimination reaction and may occur in carboxylic acid derivatives such as chlorides, esters or anhydrides. This reaction is often catalyzed by acids or bases, where the acids increase by the electrophilicity of the carbonyl group by binding to the oxygen atom, whereas the bases enhance the nucleophilicity of the attacking nucleophile.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="center"><div class="thumb tnone"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:502px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:H-Add-El.Mechanismus.PNG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="561" data-file-width="2078" decoding="async" height="135" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/H-Add-El.Mechanismus.PNG/500px-H-Add-El.Mechanismus.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/H-Add-El.Mechanismus.PNG/750px-H-Add-El.Mechanismus.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/H-Add-El.Mechanismus.PNG/1000px-H-Add-El.Mechanismus.PNG 2x" width="500"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:H-Add-El.Mechanismus.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Acid-catalyzed addition-elimination mechanism</div></div></div></div>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Nucleophilic_addition" title="Nucleophilic addition">Nucleophilic addition</a> of a <a href="/wiki/Carbanion" title="Carbanion">carbanion</a> or another <a href="/wiki/Nucleophile" title="Nucleophile">nucleophile</a> to the double bond of an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%CE%91,%CE%B2-unsaturated_carbonyl_compound" title="Α,β-unsaturated carbonyl compound">alpha, beta unsaturated carbonyl compound</a> can proceed via the <a href="/wiki/Michael_reaction" title="Michael reaction">Michael reaction</a>, which belongs to the larger class of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conjugate_addition" title="Conjugate addition">conjugate additions</a>. This is one of the most useful methods for the mild formation of C–C bonds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some additions which can not be executed with nucleophiles and electrophiles, can be succeeded with free radicals. As with the free-radical substitution, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radical_addition" title="Radical addition">radical addition</a> proceeds as a chain reaction, and such reactions are the basis of the <a href="/wiki/Radical_polymerization" title="Radical polymerization">free-radical polymerization</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_organic_reaction_mechanisms">Other organic reaction mechanisms</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cope_Rearrangement_Scheme.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="183" data-file-width="900" decoding="async" height="45" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Cope_Rearrangement_Scheme.png/220px-Cope_Rearrangement_Scheme.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Cope_Rearrangement_Scheme.png/330px-Cope_Rearrangement_Scheme.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Cope_Rearrangement_Scheme.png/440px-Cope_Rearrangement_Scheme.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cope_Rearrangement_Scheme.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Cope rearrangement of 3-methyl-1,5-hexadiene</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:224px;max-width:224px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:222px;max-width:222px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Diels_Alder_Mechanismus.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="142" data-file-width="407" decoding="async" height="77" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Diels_Alder_Mechanismus.svg/220px-Diels_Alder_Mechanismus.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Diels_Alder_Mechanismus.svg/330px-Diels_Alder_Mechanismus.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Diels_Alder_Mechanismus.svg/440px-Diels_Alder_Mechanismus.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Mechanism of a Diels-Alder reaction</div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:222px;max-width:222px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Diels_Alder_Orbitale.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="337" data-file-width="496" decoding="async" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Diels_Alder_Orbitale.svg/220px-Diels_Alder_Orbitale.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Diels_Alder_Orbitale.svg/330px-Diels_Alder_Orbitale.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Diels_Alder_Orbitale.svg/440px-Diels_Alder_Orbitale.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Orbital overlap in a Diels-Alder reaction</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>In a <a href="/wiki/Rearrangement_reaction" title="Rearrangement reaction">rearrangement reaction</a>, the carbon skeleton of a <a href="/wiki/Molecule" title="Molecule">molecule</a> is rearranged to give a <a href="/wiki/Structural_isomer" title="Structural isomer">structural isomer</a> of the original molecule. These include <a href="/wiki/Sigmatropic_reaction" title="Sigmatropic reaction">hydride shift</a> reactions such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wagner-Meerwein_rearrangement" title="Wagner-Meerwein rearrangement">Wagner-Meerwein rearrangement</a>, where a <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alkyl" title="Alkyl">alkyl</a> or <a href="/wiki/Aryl" title="Aryl">aryl</a> group migrates from one carbon to a neighboring carbon. Most rearrangements are associated with the breaking and formation of new carbon-carbon bonds. Other examples are <a href="/wiki/Sigmatropic_reaction" title="Sigmatropic reaction">sigmatropic reaction</a> such as the <a href="/wiki/Cope_rearrangement" title="Cope rearrangement">Cope rearrangement</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p><p>Cyclic rearrangements include <a href="/wiki/Cycloaddition" title="Cycloaddition">cycloadditions</a> and, more generally, <a href="/wiki/Pericyclic_reaction" title="Pericyclic reaction">pericyclic reactions</a>, wherein two or more double bond-containing molecules form a cyclic molecule. An important example of cycloaddition reaction is the <a href="/wiki/Diels%E2%80%93Alder_reaction" title="Diels–Alder reaction">Diels–Alder reaction</a> (the so-called [4+2] cycloaddition) between a conjugated <a href="/wiki/Diene" title="Diene">diene</a> and a substituted <a href="/wiki/Alkene" title="Alkene">alkene</a> to form a substituted <a href="/wiki/Cyclohexene" title="Cyclohexene">cyclohexene</a> system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p><p>Whether a certain cycloaddition would proceed depends on the electronic orbitals of the participating species, as only orbitals with the same sign of <a href="/wiki/Wave_function" title="Wave function">wave function</a> will overlap and interact constructively to form new bonds. Cycloaddition is usually assisted by light or heat. These perturbations result in different arrangement of electrons in the excited state of the involved molecules and therefore in different effects. For example, the [4+2] Diels-Alder reactions can be assisted by heat whereas the [2+2] cycloaddition is selectively induced by light.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> Because of the orbital character, the potential for developing <a href="/wiki/Stereochemistry" title="Stereochemistry">stereoisomeric</a> products upon cycloaddition is limited, as described by the <a href="/wiki/Woodward%E2%80%93Hoffmann_rules" title="Woodward–Hoffmann rules">Woodward–Hoffmann rules</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biochemical_reactions">Biochemical reactions</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:382px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Induced_fit_diagram.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="253" data-file-width="648" decoding="async" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Induced_fit_diagram.svg/380px-Induced_fit_diagram.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Induced_fit_diagram.svg/570px-Induced_fit_diagram.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Induced_fit_diagram.svg/760px-Induced_fit_diagram.svg.png 2x" width="380"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Induced_fit_diagram.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Illustration of the induced fit model of enzyme activity</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Biochemistry" title="Biochemistry">Biochemical reactions</a> are mainly controlled by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Enzymes" title="Enzymes">enzymes</a>. These <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a> can specifically <a href="/wiki/Enzyme_catalysis" title="Enzyme catalysis">catalyze</a> a single reaction, so that reactions can be controlled very precisely. The reaction takes place in the <a href="/wiki/Active_site" title="Active site">active site</a>, a small part of the enzyme which is usually found in a cleft or pocket lined by <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">amino acid</a> residues, and the rest of the enzyme is used mainly for stabilization. The catalytic action of enzymes relies on several mechanisms including the molecular shape ("induced fit"), bond strain, proximity and orientation of molecules relative to the enzyme, proton donation or withdrawal (acid/base catalysis), electrostatic interactions and many others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p><p>The biochemical reactions that occur in living organisms are collectively known as <a href="/wiki/Metabolism" title="Metabolism">metabolism</a>. Among the most important of its mechanisms is the <a href="/wiki/Anabolism" title="Anabolism">anabolism</a>, in which different <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> and enzyme-controlled processes result in the production of large molecules such as <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbohydrates" title="Carbohydrates">carbohydrates</a> from smaller units.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bioenergetics" title="Bioenergetics">Bioenergetics</a> studies the sources of energy for such reactions. Important energy sources are <a href="/wiki/Glucose" title="Glucose">glucose</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dioxygen_in_biological_reactions" title="Dioxygen in biological reactions">oxygen</a>, which can be produced by plants via <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesis</a> or assimilated from food and air, respectively. All organisms use this energy to produce <a href="/wiki/Adenosine_triphosphate" title="Adenosine triphosphate">adenosine triphosphate</a> (ATP), which can then be used to energize other reactions.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">Applications</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Velp-thermitewelding-1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="450" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Velp-thermitewelding-1.jpg/220px-Velp-thermitewelding-1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Velp-thermitewelding-1.jpg/330px-Velp-thermitewelding-1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Velp-thermitewelding-1.jpg/440px-Velp-thermitewelding-1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Velp-thermitewelding-1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Thermite reaction proceeding in railway welding. Shortly after this, the liquid iron flows into the mould around the rail gap.</div></div></div>
<p>Chemical reactions are central to <a href="/wiki/Chemical_engineering" title="Chemical engineering">chemical engineering</a>, where they are used for the synthesis of new compounds from natural raw materials such as <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">petroleum</a>, mineral <a href="/wiki/Ore" title="Ore">ores</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> in air. It is essential to make the reaction as efficient as possible, maximizing the yield and minimizing the amount of reagents, energy inputs and waste. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catalyst" title="Catalyst">Catalysts</a> are especially helpful for reducing the energy required for the reaction and increasing its <a href="/wiki/Reaction_rate" title="Reaction rate">reaction rate</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some specific reactions have their niche applications. For example, the <a href="/wiki/Thermite" title="Thermite">thermite</a> reaction is used to generate light and heat in <a href="/wiki/Pyrotechnics" title="Pyrotechnics">pyrotechnics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Welding" title="Welding">welding</a>. Although it is less controllable than the more conventional <a href="/wiki/Oxy-fuel_welding_and_cutting" title="Oxy-fuel welding and cutting">oxy-fuel welding</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arc_welding" title="Arc welding">arc welding</a> and <a href="/wiki/Flash_welding" title="Flash welding">flash welding</a>, it requires much less equipment and is still used to mend rails, especially in remote areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Monitoring">Monitoring</span></h2>
<p>Mechanisms of monitoring chemical reactions depend strongly on the reaction rate. Relatively slow processes can be analyzed in situ for the concentrations and identities of the individual ingredients. Important tools of real time analysis are the measurement of <a href="/wiki/PH" title="PH">pH</a> and analysis of optical absorption (color) and emission spectra. A less accessible but rather efficient method is introduction of a radioactive isotope into the reaction and monitoring how it changes over time and where it moves to; this method is often used to analyze redistribution of substances in the human body. Faster reactions are usually studied with <a href="/wiki/Ultrafast_laser_spectroscopy" title="Ultrafast laser spectroscopy">ultrafast laser spectroscopy</a> where utilization of <a href="/wiki/Femtochemistry" title="Femtochemistry">femtosecond</a> <a href="/wiki/Laser" title="Laser">lasers</a> allows short-lived transition states to be monitored at time scaled down to a few femtoseconds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span></h2>
